Phase I Variables (polity-based)[edit]

General variables[edit]

♠ RA ♣ Edward A L Turner; Agathe Dupreyon ♥ General description written by AD.

♠ Expert ♣ ♥


♠ UTM zone ♣ 30 P; 30 Q ♥

♠ Original name ♣ Songhai Empire - Askiya Dynasty ♥ [1]

♠ Alternative names ♣ Askiya Dynasty ♥

♠ Peak Date ♣ 1550 CE ♥

According to Kati, Askia Bunkan (1531-1537 CE) embellished court life: 'He increased the number of orchestras and singers of both sexes and lavished more favors and gifts. During his reign prosperity spread throughout his empire and an era of wealth began to be established.'[2]

Songhai height in the 16th century [3]

Askia Daud (r.1549-1582 CE) "was widely praised for memorizing the Quran and for supporting learning and religion. As part of this support, he is said to have established public libraries in his kingdom." [4]

Temporal bounds[edit]

♠ Duration ♣ 1493-1591 CE ♥

Origins of Songhay people at Gao c7th century when they displaced the Sorko, and their capital was at Kukya. Rulers converted to Islam - perhaps influenced by Berber traders - beginning 11th century, possibly 1010 CE, and then capital transferred to Gao.[5] Gao was a centre for trans-Saharan trade even before 1000 CE and had been a state well before the establishment of the Songhai empire.[6]

"In methods of government, it seems that the new Songhay leadership mainly took over the old Malian system, and this tendency became clearer when, soon after the death of Sonni Ali, power was seized by one of his generals, the Askiya Muhammad Ture, whose name would strongly suggest that he was not of Songhay but of Soninke (i.e., northern Mande) origin, and that his coup d'etat represented a return to Mande leadership in what was predominantly a Mande-speaking empire." [7]

Conquered by Morocco 1591 CE. [8]

The Songhay Empire collapsed when it was invaded by the army of the Sultan of Morocco [9].

♠ Degree of centralization ♣ unitary state ♥

♠ Supra-polity relations ♣ alliance ♥

Alliance: "Relations with the Tuareg and the Sanhaja were restored, and through them Songhay established virtual control over the salt mines of Taghaza and the copper mines of Takedda, which were the keys to the successful working of the long-distane trade." [10]

Map[edit]


General Description[edit]

Third of the great West African empires, the Songhay Empire emerged from a small kingdom based in the Gao region, which was a tributary to the Mali Empire until it started to gain autonomy in the late 14th century CE.[15] A Songhay leader named Sonyi Ali Beeri was responsible for transforming this polity into an expansionary empire from the late 15th century onwards.[16] The heyday of the Songhay Empire was under the Askiya (or Askia) dynasty, 1493‒1591. These kings consolidated Songhay power by building on the legacy of their Malinke predecessors and took control of more territories, extending their reach over the Niger Inland Delta, westward to the Atlantic ocean, northward to the salt pans of Taghaza, and eastward to the Tuareg kingdom of Agadez.[17][18][19] However, the empire was brought to an abrupt end in the late 16th century: after a succession crisis which sparked a civil war, the Sultan of Morocco invaded in 1591.[20][21]

Population and political organization

Unlike the preceding Ghana and Mali Empires, Songhay operated as a centralized unitary state.[22] The king was a revered figure but his authority was tempered by the precepts of Islam from the 11th century, and this religion became increasingly prevalent under the Askiya dynasty.[23] The imperial council coordinated the activities of the central government, which was divided into ministries including those of agriculture, finance, the army and the naval fleet.[24] The two major provinces, Kurmina in the west and Dendi in the southeast, were ruled by princes who were responsible for their own armies.[25] Thriving trading towns like Jenné, Timbuktu, Teghazza and Walata enjoyed a certain degree of autonomy due to the power of guilds and local chiefs, but had to report to a superintendent, tax inspectors, customs officials and other state appointees.[26] Vassal and tributary countries also bowed before the power of the Askiya when disputes arose.[27]
The Songhay empire is associated with the establishment of high centres of learning in Jenné, Dia, Gao and Timbuktu. The latter in particular was famed for its university, holy men, doctors and teachers, who contributed to the spread of Islamic humanism among the urban elite in the region from the 15th century.[28] The rural Songhay continued to venerate a pantheon of divinities and local spirits until Islam penetrated the countryside via the peaceful incursions of traders and government-sponsored marabouts.[29]
It is difficult to find substantiated population estimates for the Songhay Empire, but one scholar believes there could have been 70,000 people living in the city of Timbuktu by 1580 under Askiya Daoud.[30]

Social Scale[edit]

♠ Polity territory ♣ [600,000-700,000] ♥ in squared kilometers

"Songhay and tributary states" [32] - for this period including also the Mande tributary states.

Territory expanded westward under Muhammad Ture (1493-1528 CE).[33]

Askia Muhammed Toure (r.1493-1529 CE) conquered more territories beyond the Niger Inland Delta, "eastward to the Tuareg kingdom of Agadez" and "northward to the salt pans of Taghaza" [34]

"The Songhai Empire extended from east of the Niger River as far as the Atlantic Ocean and 'from the frontiers of the land of Bindoko as far as Teghezza and its dependencies' under Askia Mohammed."[35]

♠ Polity Population ♣ [4,000,000-5,000,000] ♥ People. Songhai Empire covered a similar area to the Mali Empire apart from the West African coast (and inland).

Niane had 40-50 million for the Mali Empire.[36] -- check (is reference correct? was it 4-5 million?). Yes, reference accurately reported. However, it might be a typographical error. Population of Mali in 1960 was 5 million. No references in literature to massive population crash or genocide in the region between middle ages and 1960.

McEvedy and Jones have the region of "The Sahel States" (Mauritania, Mali, Niger and Chad) at 2 million in 1000 CE, rising slowly to 3 million in 1500 CE. [37]

♠ Population of the largest settlement ♣ 60,000: 1512 CE; 70,000: 1580 CE ♥ Inhabitants.

Gao in 16th C

Mahmoud Kati explains how the inhabitants of Gao counted the number of houses in the 16th century, finding 7,626 houses, which gives a total population estimate of more than 100,000 inhabitants. [38]
"Under the Askia El Hadj a census taken by a group of students which lasted three days established that Gao consisted of 7,626 blocks of houses of solid construction (clay?) not counting straw huts."[39]

Timbuktu in 1580 CE

Timbuktu probably didn't have more than 15,000 inhabitants by the end of Sonni Ali's reign. In 1580, at the end of Askia Daoud's reign, it had gone over the 70,000 inhabitants mark. "Elle ne dépassait probablement guère 15 000 habitants à la fin du règne de Sonni Ali. En 1580, à la fin du règne d'Askia Daoud, elle était passée à plus de 70 000 habitants." [40]

Djenne in 1512 CE

In 1512, Niani still had about 60,000 inhabitants (6,000 households), as told by Leo Africanus. [41]

Within the Mande-speaking heartland the basic building-block of government was the kafu, a community of anything from 1000 to 15,000 people living in or near a mud-walled town and ruled by a hereditary dynast called a fama." [42]

♠ Largest communication distance ♣ 900 ♥ in kilometers.

Gao to Segu (or Niani? 1333 km2 or Timbuktu? 220 km2).

kings called dias until c1335 CE. After that titles were sunni or shi.[48]


__Court Bureaucracy__

Askia Muhammad Toure "supported by Mande clans ... created a standing army and a central bureaucracy."[49]

"The Songhay empire, like that of Mali before it thus involved a gigantic effort of state enterprise in production and trade as well as in military operations and civil government."[50]

Slave colonies

"the Songhay empire depended greatly on its colonies of royal slaves and on its privileged castes of craftsmen, which had probably been built up originally from the more skilled groups of war captives, such as smiths, weavers and leather-workers. Here again, Songhay took over a system already initiated in Mali, while adding greatly to the numbers of slaves by means of the regular annual raids carried out by the Songhay cavalry among the unprotected, stateless peoples living south of the Niger bend." [61]
3. State farms manager (possibly 4. if responsible to an official from the court)
State farms "were spread right across the empire, to supply the government and the garrisons, but the largest concentration was still to be found in the well-watered inland delta" - a lot of this grain went to the towns, desert caravans and salt mines. [62]
4. Lower-level official


__Regional government__

2. Western Songhai (Mande speakers) - rule based in Timbuktu?
"Not under Muhammad only, but also under the succession of sons and grandsons who followed him as Akiyas until 1591, the real thrust of Songhay was toward the west and the north. It was an impetus based upon Timbuktu, both as the centre of Islamic learning in the western Sudan and as the meeting-point of river and desert communications.[63]
Askia Muhammad divided the army into two parts: "one for the western provinces based in Timbuktu and one for the eastern provinces based in Gao." [64]

"Under Askia Muhammad, the Songhai Empire established lands in which the kings paid tribute." [65]

♠ Religious levels ♣ 3 ♥ levels.

Askia Mohammed requested a "Sherif, i.e., the descendant of the Prophet" to be sent to live with in the Sudan. Caliph Mulay Abbas obliged and the Sherif became an important figure, "exempt from all the duties of citizenship (taxes, etc.), but they received gifts of impressive value."[73]

1. Sherif

"The Sherifs, in oder to hold and increase their prestige, make consummate use of drugs (opium and hashish) which they discreetly mix with tobacco for smoking or give to their followers (the talebs) to chew. This gives rise to wonderful visions. The believer who comes back to his senses when the effects of the drug wear off is thus convinced that the gates of heaven were opened to him for a moment, and that he was thus miraculously, divinely transported to paradise."[74]

1. Caliph of the Land of Takrur

The sharif of Mecca made Askiya Muhammad Toure "caliph of the land of Takrur" however the masses were still pagan [75]
Askiya Muhammad Toure made Islam the official state religion, "built mosques, and brought Muslim scholars ... to Gao." [76]

3.


♠ Military levels ♣ [5-6] ♥ levels.

Songhay Empire: Askia Muhammad had a full time general called dyini-koy or balama.[77]

1. King

In Mali and Songhai "the king appinted the generals was himself commander-in-chief of the army and personally directed military operations"[78]
2. General of the armies (Djima koi)
3. Corps
"In each kingdom, each nation, the army was divided into several corps assigned to the defense of different provinces, although under the command of the civil authority. Thus, each provincial governor had at his disposal a part of this army which he could assign tasks under the orders of a general whose powers were purely military."In Mali and Songhai "the king appinted the generals was himself commander-in-chief of the army and personally directed military operations"[79]
4. officer ranks
5. officer ranks
6. Individual soldier

Divisions of army: "knights, cavalry, footsoldiers, auxiliary bodies of Tuaregs, elite infantry regiments, the royal guard, and an armed flotilla."[80]

Tunkoi, kuran, soira: subaltern military positions in city such as Djenne.[81]

Djenne-koi, Bani-koi, Kora-koi: "administrative and military chiefs of cities and regions; they thus had under their command a territorial guard."[82]

Wars of Muhammad Toure (1493-1528 CE), according to himself, "were undertaken to distract the Songhay-speaking element in his armies from meddling in the Mande-speaking western half of his empire where his own interests were strongest, and where he preferred to rule through slave armies recruited from his own war captives." [83]

♠ Occupational complexity ♣ ♥

Occupations

ironworking, woodworking, pottery, weaving, dying cloth, masonry. [89]
"The gesere who played music and narrated traditional legends were among the occupational specialists. In the days of the Askias of Songhay, the chief gesere had the title gesere-dunka."[90]

♠ Professional military officers ♣ present ♥ Askia Muhammed Toure (r.1493-1529 CE) "supported by Mande clans ... created a standing army"[91] Askia Muhammed Toure (r.1493-1529 CE) "created a professional full-time army" [92] General of the armies: Djima koï. Head of cavalry in the event of conflict: governor of Dirma (one of his many duties).[93] before Askia Muhammad "Chiefs, kings and emperors of earlier times had relied on simply 'calling up' their subjects, their vassals, or their allies. ... But these were temporary armies. They were amateur armies. They served for a campaign or a war, and then everyone went home again until the next one."[94]

♠ Professional soldiers ♣ inferred present ♥ Askia Muhammed Toure (r.1493-1529 CE) "supported by Mande clans ... created a standing army"[95] Askia Muhammed Toure (r.1493-1529 CE) "created a professional full-time army" [96] before Askia Muhammad "Chiefs, kings and emperors of earlier times had relied on simply 'calling up' their subjects, their vassals, or their allies. ... But these were temporary armies. They were amateur armies. They served for a campaign or a war, and then everyone went home again until the next one."[97]

Distinction between people and army "beginning with the reign of Askia Mohammad ... Instead of mass conscription, a permanent army was created; civilians who were not part of it could go about their business."[98] Previously,uUnder Sonni Ali, "all able-bodied nationals were subject to enlistment."[99]

Bureaucracy characteristics[edit]

♠ Full-time bureaucrats ♣ present ♥ Central government divided into ministries.[102]

The king's top officials included a finance officer (katisi-farma) he had sub-officials.[103] There were many other offices in the Songhai government.[104]

Letter writing was common means of communication.[105] Askia Mohammed had a secretary who could draw up written documents.[106]

♠ Source of support ♣ state salary ♥ "Muslim clerics, once restored to favour, supplied the ideological support and the legal framework necessary for the efficient government of a large territory within which many people were constantly moving around outside their traditional ethnic areas." [107] Askia Muhammed Toure "appointed the first qadi of Jenne and extended Islamic judicial administration to other towns by establishing courts and appointing judges." [108]

Law[edit]

♠ Formal legal code ♣ present ♥

Askia Muhammed Toure (r.1493-1529 CE) 'appointed the first qadi of Jenne and extended Islamic judicial administration to other towns by establishing courts and appointing judges.' [110] Increasingly, law became Islamic law and a legal system developed that was independent of tribal chiefs, although customary law continued.[111]

♠ Judges ♣ present ♥ Askia Muhammed Toure (r.1493-1529 CE) "appointed the first qadi of Jenne and extended Islamic judicial administration to other towns by establishing courts and appointing judges." [112]

Kadi. Islamic law. Legal system independent of tribal chiefs. Customary law. Court to punish adultery. Tribunals. [113]

The cadi was a Muslim judge appointed by the king who "handled mainly common-law misdemeanors, disputes between citizens, or between citizens and foreigners."[114]

♠ Courts ♣ present ♥ Askia Muhammed Toure (r.1493-1529 CE) "appointed the first qadi of Jenne and extended Islamic judicial administration to other towns by establishing courts and appointing judges." [115]

Kadi. Islamic law. Legal system independent of tribal chiefs. Customary law. Court to punish adultery. Tribunals. [116]

Specialized Buildings: polity owned[edit]

♠ Communal buildings ♣ present ♥

♠ Special purpose house ♣ present ♥

♠ Utilitarian public buildings ♣ present ♥ Built dykes and encouraged agriculture. [117]

♠ irrigation systems ♣ inferred present ♥ "Archaeological evidence affirms that the building of terraces and irrigation canals in sub-Saharan Africa pre-dates external influence..." [118]
♠ drinking water supply systems ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ markets ♣ present ♥ Weekly local or village markets were the center of a remarkable system of exchange and distribution for great quantities of food and other types of products. "Les marchés locaux ou marchés de village se tenant une fois par semaine étaint des centres d'un remarquable système d'échange et de distribution de grandes quantités de denrées alimentaires, et de produits de toutes sortes." [119] There were also interregional markets. [120] Description of the merchant class and their exchange activities in the cities (mainlt Djenné and Timbuktu), on rivers and in caravans [121] polity owed? An official called the Yobu-koi "was in charge of the market."[122]
♠ food storage sites ♣ present ♥ State farms "were spread right across the empire, to supply the government and the garrisons, but the largest concentration was still to be found in the well-watered inland delta" [123] Agricultural products from plantations "stored in clay granaies used for purposes of silage."[124]

♠ Symbolic buildings ♣ present ♥


♠ Entertainment buildings ♣ ♥ unknown

Daud was a patron of the arts, gave welfare to poor [125]

♠ Knowledge/information buildings ♣ present ♥

"Askia Daud was widely praised for memorizing the Quran and for supporting learning and religion. As part of this support, he is said to have established public libraries in his kingdom." [126]

University of Timbuktu. "It included a large number of independent schools, particularly the famous mosque of Sankore, which provided a higher education ... The Sudanese university, like all those in the Muslim world at that time, taught the humanities, which included the traditional academic subjects - theology (tawhïd), exegesis (tafsir), traditions (hadtth) and Mälikite jurisprudence (fikh) - as well as grammar, rhetoric, logic, astrology, astronomy, history and geography. Scientific and mathematical knowledge must have been very rudimentary. Mälikite law was the speciality of the doctors of Timbuktu, who are referred to throughout the Ta'rikks as 'jurisconsults'" [127]

Archives.[128]

Measurement System[edit]

♠ Volume ♣ inferred present ♥ Cowrie shells "can be accurately traded by weight, by volume, and by counting"[134] "After about AD 900 the standard measure of value in North Africa became the Almoravid dinar, sometimes in West Africa called the mitcal. Although West Africans continued to use their own standards for measuring value - the cowrie shell became the most important of these - they also began measuring in dinars or mitcals when they were trading with North Africans."[135]

Although West Africans continued to use their own standards for measuring value - the cowrie shell became the most important of these - they also began measuring in dinars or mitcals when they were trading with North Africans."[137]

Writing System[edit]

♠ Mnemonic devices ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Nonwritten records ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Written records ♣ present ♥ "The West Africans who laid the foundations of their medieval empires during the centuries before 900 C.E. did not develop a written language they could use to record historical events."[138] Written language was culture of an urban élite, that did not absorb surrounding cultures and languages [139] Oldest example of writing in West Africa c1100 CE tomb inscription at Gao.[140]
♠ Script ♣ present ♥ Written language was culture of an urban élite, that did not absorb surrounding cultures and languages [141] Oldest example of writing in West Africa c1100 CE tomb inscription at Gao.[142]
♠ Non-phonetic writing ♣ inferred absent ♥ "There are no written records of any description to throw light on the history of West Africa before 900 A.D." [143] "The West Africans who laid the foundations of their medieval empires during the centuries before 900 C.E. did not develop a written language they could use to record historical events."[144] Oldest example of writing in West Africa c1100 CE tomb inscription at Gao.[145]
♠ Phonetic alphabetic writing ♣ inferred present ♥ Classic Arabic of Koran. "There are no written records of any description to throw light on the history of West Africa before 900 A.D." [146] "The West Africans who laid the foundations of their medieval empires during the centuries before 900 C.E. did not develop a written language they could use to record historical events."[147] Oldest example of writing in West Africa c1100 CE tomb inscription at Gao.[148]

He called his book The Duties of Kings."[154]

Money[edit]

♠ Articles ♣ present ♥ Barter "at the periphery of the African kingdoms, some backwards tribes, such as the Lem-Lem in Southwest Ghana, perhaps on the banks of the present-day Faleme River, had been carrying on barter trade since the Carthaginian period."[156] This was where, without any direct contact, Carthaginian and Arab traders exchanged their goods for gold dust. However, this simple form of economy was not characteristic of the economies of the polities of these times.[157]
♠ Tokens ♣ present ♥ according to Ibn al-Mukhtar who was writing in the seventeenth century "the king would pay a dowry of 40,000 cowries to the girl's family in order to establish his right of ownership over her children" in the event she married a slave. [158] Cowrie shells "can be accurately traded by weight, by volume, and by counting; their colour and lustre do not fade as their durability compares favourably with that of metal coins."[159]
♠ Precious metals ♣ present ♥ Gold.
♠ Foreign coins ♣ inferred present ♥ Treasury of coins established from proceeds of taxation [160] Cosmopolitan commerce centers: Timbuktu, Djenne, Biru, Soo, Ndob, Pekes and some others.[161] Currency "consisted of salt, cowries, or gold in either dust or pieces (of foreign or local mintage)."[162]
♠ Indigenous coins ♣ inferred present ♥ Surprisingly the state probably did not mint coins: 'no trace of a die or mint has been found south of the desert.'[163] There were coins of gold, but they were not minted. [164] There were also rings of iron that were used to purchase cheap items. [165] Currency "consisted of salt, cowries, or gold in either dust or pieces (of foreign or local mintage)."[166] According to Leo Africanus cowries used as currency for trading came from the Indian Ocean, via Persia.[167] According to al Bakri (11th century) 'The dinars they used were of pure gold and were called sola [bald] because they bore no imprints.' ... Thus these documents allow us to be sure of the use in Black Africa of imprinted gold coins, without, however, being able to know whether such imprints were effiges of local emperors or kings, or to know whether there was any generalized imperial currency minited apart from the mitkal standard."[168]
♠ Paper currency ♣ absent ♥
♠ Debt and credit structures ♣ inferred present ♥ We do not know whether loan-sharking was widely practiced in Sudanese society then, (although everything leads us to believe it was, because of the sheer number of Jewish merchants in Gao and Timbuktu), but simple loans were common practice. "Nous ignorons si le prêt avec usure connut une grande extension dans la société soudanaise de l'époque (cependant tout le laisse croire puisqu'il y avait nombre de commerçants juifs à Gao, et à Tombouctou), toutefois le simple prêt était de pratique courante." [169] Ibn Haukal (10th century) "attests to the use of acknowledgement of debts in writing".[170]
♠ Store of wealth ♣ inferred present ♥ Gold was stored as ingots or chains. [171]

Postal System[edit]

♠ Couriers ♣ present ♥ An important courier service connected the court to various cities and towns, guaranteeing the transmission of the Askia's orders. "Un important service de courrier relie la cour aux différentes villes et chef-lieux assurant la transmission des ordres de l'Askia." [172] Letter writing was common.[173]
♠ Postal stations ♣ ♥ unknown An important courier service connected the court to various cities and towns, guaranteeing the transmission of the Askia's orders. "Un important service de courrier relie la cour aux différentes villes et chef-lieux assurant la transmission des ordres de l'Askia." [174]

♠ Fastest individual communication ♣ 8 ♥ days. 1400 km in eight days. Leo Africanus tells us that a fast courier could cover the 1400 m separating Sudan from Sidjilmassi (Morocco) in 8 days. "Léon l'Africain nous dit qu'un courrier rapide couvrait les 1400 kilomètres séparant le Soudan de Sidjilmassa en huit jours." [175]

Armor[edit]

♠ Wood, bark, etc ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Leather, cloth ♣ present ♥ there were privileged castes of craftsmen which likely included leather-workers. [187] "The Tuaregs wore puffed trousers, a tunic, a turban, and a litham."[188] 1000-1650 CE period: "body armor was rare. Among the cavalry empires of the Sahel and sudan, quilted horse and body armor were common but plate was rarely used."[189]
♠ Shields ♣ inferred present ♥ The Sudanese knight also wore an iron helmet, which made a loud clinking noise. "Le chevalier soudanais portait aussi un bouclier de fer dont le cliquetis faisait un vacarme impressionant." [190] Cavalry and footsoldiers had shields.[191]
♠ Helmets ♣ inferred present ♥ The songhay knight, like his French counterpart, wore a great helm, a hauberk, chainmail, an iron plate armour, a helmet and used a javelin. "Le chevalier songhoy, comme son homologue de l'Ile de France, portait heaume et haubert, cottes de mailles, cuirasse de fer, casque et javelot." [192]
♠ Breastplates ♣ present ♥ Knights: "The princes of Black Africa who could afford to outfitted themselves in complete or partial armor like that of the knights of the Western Middle Ages."[193] "coat of mail and iron breastplate, helmet, boots, javelin ... all of it."[194] However, due to climate complete knightly armour not as common as in Europe and in fact Songhai Askia Bano died of suffocation.[195] 1000-1650 CE period: "body armor was rare. Among the cavalry empires of the Sahel and sudan, quilted horse and body armor were common but plate was rarely used."[196]

Fortifications[edit]

♠ Settlements in a defensive position ♣ inferred present ♥ In the 15th century, Djenné was the archetype of a fortified city: built on an island, it was defended by a ring of water; the city itself was protected by a wall with 11 doors. "Mais au XVè siècle, Djenné était la ville forte par excellence: bâtie sur une île, elle était admirablement défendue par une ceinture d'eau; la ville elle-même était protégée par une enceinte percée de 11 portes." [210]

Other technologies[edit]

Military Organization[edit]

♠ Standing armies ♣ present ♥ Askia Muhammed Toure (r.1493-1529 CE) "supported by Mande clans ... created a standing army"[214] Askia Muhammed Toure (r.1493-1529 CE) "created a professional full-time army" [215] before Askia Muhammad "Chiefs, kings and emperors of earlier times had relied on simply 'calling up' their subjects, their vassals, or their allies. ... But these were temporary armies. They were amateur armies. They served for a campaign or a war, and then everyone went home again until the next one."[216]

♠ Uniformity of equipment ♣ unknown ♥

♠ Total army ♣ 100,000 ♥ is the number of men that could be gathered, and the imperial cavalry was constituted of 40,000 men [217] Mali: could gather 100,000 men and the imperial cavalry reached 40,000 men. The king of Djoloff (Jalofo), vassal to the Songhay : 10,000 mounted men and 100 000 foot soldiers, without any armour (fought naked), but according to Valentin Fernandez there were only 8,000 in the cavalry. Bornou: several thousand men. Gao: four military provinces, each of them had a civil and a military governor. Timbuktu had a garrison of 3000 men and many foot soldiers. In 1570, Askia Daoud's son gathered 24,000 men from the El-Hamdiya. In 1591, Askia Ishaq II gathered 30,000. The Songhay kings never had to gather all troops. [218]

♠ Professional army ♣ 100,000 ♥

♠ Largest fielded army ♣ ♥ In 1591, Askia Ishaq II gathered 30,000 men. The Songhay never needed to gather all troops. [219] - possibly the largest fielded army?

General Characteristics of Warfare[edit]

Types of Warfare[edit]

♠ Inter-polity wars ♣ present ♥ Raids on Borgu, in the west of modern Nigeria, the Hausa states of Zaria and Kano, and the city of Agades in modern Niger. Invaded by Morocco 1591 CE. Tuareg kingdom of Agadez.

Attacked Borgu 1505 CE.[221]

Expedition against Diara 1512 CE.[222]

♠ Internal wars ♣ present ♥ "The Hausa states revolted against Songhay overlordship during the reign of Muhammad II."[223]

♠ Intra-elite conflicts ♣ present ♥ Civil war c1531 CE. Civil war 1582 CE. Civil war c1588 CE.

♠ Popular uprisings ♣ inferred present ♥ Peasant revolts and seditions within the troops originated from the excesses of high-ranking officials from 1580 onwards. "Les révoltes des paysans, les séditions dans les troupes eurent pour cause, à partir de 1580, les abus des hauts-fonctionnaires." [224]

♠ Military revolts ♣ present ♥ Military rebellion against Askia Ishaq II 1591 CE.

Sunni Ali was replaced by his son Sunni Baru who was deposed by a rebel called Muhammad Turay (Askia the Great).[225]

♠ Separatist rebellions ♣ inferred present ♥ In 1563, Askia Daoud organized a secret expedition against a rebellious general. [...] The troops used a non-traditional mountain path and surprised the enemy, completely overtaking his forces. "Askia Daoud, en 1563, envoya une expédition secrète contre un général rebelle; seul le général Bokar détenait le secret, même le fils du souverain, qui faisait partie de l'expédition, en ignorait le but: les troupes abandonnant les routes traditionnelles passèrent par la montage et surprirent l'ennemi. La déroute de celui-ci fut complète." [226] The military campaign lasted for 13 months and the enemies of the Songhay were massacred. [227]

"The Hausa states revolted against Songhay overlordship during the reign of Muhammad II."[228]

Intensity of warfare[edit]

♠ Annexation ♣ present ♥ No data yet found in the literature.

♠ Looting ♣ inferred present ♥ Expedition to fight Mossi in 1505 CE: "he brought some of their children to Kukiya to be raised as Muslims."[230] People as loot.

♠ Sack ♣ suspected unknown ♥ No data yet found in the literature.

♠ Rape ♣ suspected unknown ♥ No data yet found in the literature.

♠ Deportation ♣ present ♥ [The prisoners] usually became slaves and were settled into villages; thus, because of warfare entire populations were relocated. Askia Mohammed started this practice: the pagans of the Tombo and the Hombori as well as the Bambaras were captured during a raid and resettled into royal plantations. "Ils étaient plutôt réduits en esclavage et installés dans des villages de culture; il y eut ainsi par le fait de la guerre de véritables transferts de populations. C'est Askia Mohammed qui en donna le ton; les païens du Tombo, du Hombori et les Bambaras furent razziés en masse et installés dans les plantations royales."[231]

♠ Expulsion ♣ present ♥ Sonni Ali Ber captured Timbuktu. 1468 CE: "scattering the Tamaschek administration and taking over the lucrative trading houses."[232]

♠ Enslavement ♣ present ♥ Expedition to fight Mossi in 1505 CE: "he brought some of their children to Kukiya to be raised as Muslims."[233]

Defeated peoples were treated as subjects or as slaves.[234]

♠ Imprisonment ♣ present ♥ "Soon the same Islamic force that had been used to overthrow the father, the power of the ulama, was used by Is'mail to overthrow Musa in 1537. Askia Is'mail freed his father from the captivity that had been imposed on him by Musa but did not give up the throne."[235]

Ritual variables[edit]

A collective ritual is a ritual in which two or more people participate. With the official cult we refer to the set of collective ritual practices that are most closely associated with legitimation of the power structure (including elites, if any).

♠ RA ♣ Enrico Cioni ♥

♠ Expert ♣ ♥

Animist cult to the ancestors still practiced during the 16th century. [238]

Largest scale collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Hajj ♥ "In the pilgrimage to Mecca, the hajj, ancient Arab rites have been taken over and spiritualized. The pilgrimage is performed during the last lunar month, Dhu'l­hijja. People who come to Arabia at other times can perform the lesser pilgrimage, umra, which consists mainly of the circumambulation of the Ka'ba and the running between two hillocks called Safa and Marwa. Pilgrimage to a sacred place requires a ritual consecration. Thus, at a certain distance from Mecca the men, with shaven heads, put on a special garment, ihram, consisting of two white unsewn pieces of cloth that cover their whole body; women put on a covering garment. Pilgrims greet the sacred precincts with the call labbaika, "Here I am at Thy service!" The rites that have to be performed include the sevenfold circumambulation of the Ka'ba (where one tries to kiss the black stone in its southeastern corner) and a sevenfold running between Safa and Marwa, which are now connected by covered arcades. But the central rite is the "staying" on the hill 'Arafat*, a distance of some eight miles from Mecca, on the ninth day of the month. There, sermons are given, and on the way back the 'stoning of Satan' is performed by casting seven pebbles three times at a certain place. After that, on the tenth day of the month, each pilgrim slaughters a sheep or larger animal in Mina. Those staying home do the same. This part of the ritual commemorates the day when Abraham was ordered to sacrifice his son Isma'il, whose place was then taken by a ram. After the Feast of Offering the pilgrim can return from the sacred state, ihram, into the normal state, when the cutting of hair and nails, as well as sex, are again permitted. Killing any creatures inside the sacred area is considered a major sin. The pilgrimage should be performed only when the pilgrim is in good health and can undertake the journey without major difficulties, especially without incurring debts; this shows the realistic approach of Islam to religious practice. In former centuries the pilgrimage was a dangerous undertaking for those who came from distant lands, whether they traveled overland in the caravans which started, with great pomp, in Cairo or Baghdad, or sailed from India or Malaysia on fragile boats. (Air travel is not always very safe either.) Yet modern Muslims sometimes express the view that these slow, arduous journeys prepared them better for the great event than the brief trip by air, which barely leaves leisure to meditate on the experience of visiting the center of their religion. On their return the pilgrims bring a vessel filled with wholesome water from the well Zamzam, from which Hagar once drank. They are received by their families with pride and joy, and are called Hajji. Those who die during the pilgrimage are regarded as martyrs, shahid, "witness" for their faith. Doubtlessly the meeting of people from all parts of the world in Mecca and their common worship at the central sanctuary of Islam does much to strengthen the feeling of unity among Muslims. Through the centuries many scholars and pious people extended their stay in Mecca and exchanged their religious views with colleagues from other parts of the world. The sojourn near the Ka'ba inspired important theological and mystical works: the comprehensive commentary on the Koran by Zamakhshari (called by the honorific title Jar Allah, "God's neighbor" due to his prolonged stay in Mecca) grew out of the author's stay in Mecca. Even more evident is the influence of the Meccan experience on the most comprehensive work of Islamic theosophy, the Futuhat al­makkiyya, of Ibn 'Arabi, which was "revealed" to the author while he was circumambulating the Ka'ba. Furthermore, it is important to remember that almost all reform movements in the fringe areas of the Islamic world were triggered off by the pilgrimage. Faithful believers from Bengal and Nigeria, from Morocco and Central Asia, who had lived for some time in Mecca and exchanged their views with others who, like them, had experienced what seemed to be the 'true Arabic Islam', returned to their Page 39countries to fight the numerous indigenous superstitions and customs which had infiltrated the Islam of the masses and which now appeared to them like remnants of ancient paganism."[239]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ The main acts of the Hajj take place in the twelfth month of the Islamic calendar, although "a pilgrim can start going into consecration (ihram) for Hajj earlier, from the beginning of the tenth month (Shawwal)".[240]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ once in a lifetime ♥ Every Muslim should undertake the Hajj pilgrimage at least once in their lifetime.[241]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ yearly ♥ The inhabitants of Mecca, Medina and other important waypoints along the pilgrimage may be considered as "passive spectators or witnesses", as the Seshat codebook puts it. They would witness the Hajj, or parts of it, between the tenth and the twelfth months of the Islamic calendar, every year.[242]
♠ Duration ♣ 144 ♥ hours. 6 days From the 8th to the 13th day of the last month of the Islamic calendar..[243] The main acts of the Hajj take place in five days of the twelfth month of the Islamic calendar, that is, from the 8th to the 13th of the month of Dhu'l Hijja: however, "a pilgrim can start going into consecration (ihram) for Hajj earlier, from the beginning of the tenth month (Shawwal)"[244]

Costs of participation
♠ currency ♣ present ♥ Although there was no official cost of participation, in that no money was charged for pilgrimage, to undertake hajj was an expensive occurrence which prohibited many from undertaking it. The pilgrim would need to travel to Mecca, would need food and accommodation for that time, would need money to buy animals for sacrifice..[271]
♠ property/valuable items ♣ present ♥ Though this is not prescribed by sacred texts, there is a long historical tradition of the wealthiest pilgrims distributing money and precious objects (e.g. textiles) to the less wealthy.[272]
♠ health costs ♣ present ♥ Hajj was physically demanding including a seven times repeated run between two hills and several miles trekking into the desert..[273]
♠ animal sacrifice ♣ present ♥ On the third of the five main days of the Hajj, animal sacrifice is performed.[274]

Most widespread collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Friday prayer ♥ "The Friday prayer is a duty of the community; it contains a short sermon, khutba*, which consists of two parts. It gives some advice usually based on the Koran or the hadith, and a prayer for the ruler of the government. To be mentioned in the khutba meant to be the legitimate ruler. Women rarely attend the Friday prayer but prefer to pray at home.The daily prayers are explained in the Koran as acts of humility and adoration. At the end of the prayer the Muslim can mention his/her personal requests. While traveling or in war or under duress, one can perform the noon and afternoon or sunset and night prayers together. One also has numerous opportunities to perform additional prayers of two or more rak'a each, e.g., during solar or lunar eclipses; when performing the rain prayer; when entering or leaving the house or the mosque—in short, some pious Muslims use every opportunity to perform a brief salat. The belief in the purifying power of ritual prayer is intense; the Prophet compared it to a stream of water that washes off sins five times a day. The performance of the prayer at the prescribed time constitutes ideally a means of educating Muslims to punctuality, cleanliness, and, since there is no ranking in the mosque, equal participation in the life of the community. Prayer can also lead to ecstatic experiences, and when one observes a praying Muslim who is oblivious of everything around him and seems to have drawn himself, as it were, out of this world to stand humbly before the Lord, one realizes best whence Islam derives its vital strength.To perform the salat, a clean spot suffices, be it in the fields, in the train, or in one's shop or office. A small prayer rug guarantees the cleanliness of the place. However, even in the Prophet's lifetime a place for congregational worship was established, called masjid, "the place where one prostrates" (hence the word Mosque). The great mosque, where the Friday prayer is held and which was found in each quarter, is called jami'*, "the gathering [mosque]."Mainly in the eastern part of the Islamic world, one finds small open prayer places called musalla*, and for the gathering of large congregations at the two 'id*, the end of Ramadan and the Feast of Offerings, one sometimes uses an 'idgah*, a vast enclosure with a prayer niche."[278]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ weekly ♥ Every Friday. Smaller version five times a day.[279]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ weekly ♥ Every Friday. Smaller version five times a day.[280]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ weekly ♥ Every Friday. Smaller version five times a day.[281]
♠ Duration ♣ 1 ♥ hour. Noon prayer on Fridays. Expert input needed for exact duration. at this time wiki scraper not able to read decimal, so cannot code less than 1 hour e.g. 0.05 for 15 minutes.

♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ substantial minority ♥ All Muslims, though women would frequently pray in private instead.[282]

Most euphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Id al Fitr ♥ 'Id al-Fitr is the best loved Islamic festival, and it was already an important part of the Islamic calendar by the seventh century CE [352]. Descriptions of the festival are surprisingly rare, especially as regards how it was celebrated in the past, but given that most Islamic practices demonstrate a great degree of continuity with the past, so descriptions of how the festival is celebrated in modern times may apply to how it was celebrated in the past as well. 'Id al-Fitr is celebrated at the end of the month-long Islamic fast known as Ramadan. "As in the Friday afternoon congregational prayer of the weekly Day of Assembly, the Eid morning prayers at the mosque should be preceded by a bath and attended with clean (often new) clothes. After breakfast, men, women, and children gather at an outdoor prayer meeting, the musallah, in front of a white wall adorned with a niche, the mihraab pointing in the direction of Mecca (like the one in every mosque). Women are given the option of praying at home--as they mostly do the rest of the year. The prayers consist of two units (rakat) plus the six-to-sixteen additional takbir recitations of Allahu Akbar ('God is Most Great'). These set them apart from Friday prayers, as does the fact that they are followed instead of preceded by a two-part sermon (khutba). [...] This enthusiasm erupts spontaneously on the aptly named Eid-al-Fitr, the festival of the breaking of the month-long Ramadan fast. Still, by then, passions have been so well trained that few unbridled excesses are in evidence at the happy family celebrations of warm community that follow or at the fairground attractions that are set up in some places [...]. Indeed, restored social harmony is on display everywhere over up to three days, with people greeting and congratulating each other in the streets as well as on afternoon visits to relatives, friends, and sick people. Many gifts are offered, particularly to the children of less fortunate families. At home, the family celebrates around a noontime festive meal [...]. Dead relatives are also remembered as their graves are visited that day." [353] There is also a specific type of charity given on the occasion of 'Id al-Fitr, known as Zakat-ul-Fitr [354].

♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ yearly ♥ [357]

Most dysphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ circumcision ♥ Circumcision was an important rite of passage for boys in early twentieth-century Soghay society, one that combined Muslim traditions with ones that preceded the arrival of Islam in the region, so it is reasonable to infer that it was similarly important at this time. Only through circumcision could a boy become a man, and therefore marry and join an important type of mutual aid group known as a kondey. The ceremony was rather elaborate but may be summarised as follows. The ritual occurred every few years and usually involved more than a hundred boys. One of the fathers, usually a particularly wealthy one, would organise the ceremonies and bear the brunt of the expenses, thus gaining much prestige. On a Monday, the boys gather in a specially prepared house, with their relatives, and they are treated to a feast. Then they are given charms to wear, and their hands are hennaed. There is drumming, dancing, and singing, and eventually the boys' relatives leave. Then the actual circumcision is carried out by "barbers" wearing deliberately frightening face paint. After the circumcision, the boys stay in the house for two weeks to heal, eat nice meals, and generally do as they wish. They regularly play music and sing a circumcision song. There are a couple of ceremonies in which the boys are bathed and their bandages are changed. At the end, before re-entering society, they are given many gifts. [389] NOTE: This source is quite old, but it provides an eyewitness account of the ritual as it was performed in the early twentieth century, and there is little to suggest that it is unreliable.
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ every 2-3 years . The ritual was too expensive to be performed every year [390].
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ once in a lifetime ♥ [391]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ yearly ♥ every 2-3 years. The ritual was too expensive to be performed every year [392].
♠ Duration ♣ 336 ♥ hours. Two weeks. [393]
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ [100-120] ♥ More than a hundred boys were circumcised per event [394].
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ elites; substantial minority ♥ priesthood; barbers. Besides Islamic clergy, "barbers" were involved: they practiced the actual circumcision and they looked after the boys in their two weeks of healing [395].
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ substantial minority ♥ young males. Usually before the age of ten [396].

Limits on Power of the Chief Executive[edit]

Legal (formal) limits[edit]

♠ Executive power is separate or independent from military ♣ absent ♥ "In Mali and Songhai "the king appinted the generals was himself commander-in-chief of the army and personally directed military operations".[432] Askia Muhammad of Songahai Empire was in charge of a professional military: "Chiefs, kings and emperors of earlier times had relied on simply 'calling up' their subjects, their vassals, or their allies. ... But these were temporary armies. They were amateur armies. They served for a campaign or a war, and then everyone went home again until the next one."[433]
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from legislative ♣ inferred present ♥ Author makes reference to "seat in the Assembly" in context of high officials.[434] "Islam provided the kind of laws that were useful to bigger states and larger market-towns. Such laws were about the administration of justice, the inheritance of property, the improvement of market organization".[435] Askia Mohammed requested a "Sherif, i.e., the descendant of the Prophet" to be sent to live with in the Sudan. Caliph Mulay Abbas obliged and the Sherif became an important figure, "exempt from all the duties of citizenship (taxes, etc.), but they received gifts of impressive value."[436]
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from judiciary ♣ present ♥ For preceding Mali Empire we have al-Omari of Cairo who noted that Mansa Kankan Musa of Mali (1412-1337 CE) received legal cases and appeals and 'examined them himself'.[437] However, it seems that judicial power at the highest level may have been split. "Crimes of lese-majeste and high treason were under the jurisdiction of the king."[438] The cadi, a Muslim judge appointed by the king, "handled mainly common-law misdemeanors, disputes between citizens, or between citizens and foreigners."[439] Underscoring the independence of the judiciary, "A judge's house and a mosque were inviolable sanctuaries. The cadi could with impunity admonish the king; to the princes, he was the respected intercessor whose words were heeded."[440]

Legal (formal) freedoms[edit]

♠ Right to petition executive ♣ inferred present ♥
♠ Critique of executive by government ♣ present ♥ "When Askia Ishaq II faced the Moroccan army at the disastrous battle of Tondibi in 1591, his chief secretary and adviser, Alfa Askia Lanbar, actually persuaded him to abandon the field."[441] The barei-koi was an official who was "the chief of etiquette and protocol."[442] "A judge's house and a mosque were inviolable sanctuaries. The cadi could with impunity admonish the king; to the princes, he was the respected intercessor whose words were heeded."[443]
♠ Critique of executive by non-government ♣ inferred present ♥ non-Muslim people in the countryside, people of the towns (i.e. merchant class?) "Unlike his father, Sunni Baru refused to declare himself a Muslim and at once made it clear that he was going to side entirely with the non-Muslim people in the countryside. ... But the people of the towns were too powerful to be treated in this way. They feared that they would lose power and influence, and that their trade would suffer. They found a rebellious leader in Muhammad Turay."[444]

Power distributed[edit]

♠ Sanction of executive by government ♣ inferred absent ♥
♠ Sanction of executive by non-government ♣ inferred absent ♥
♠ Constraint on executive by government ♣ inferred present ♥ The independence of the judiciary, "A judge's house and a mosque were inviolable sanctuaries. The cadi could with impunity admonish the king; to the princes, he was the respected intercessor whose words were heeded."[445]
♠ Constraint on executive by non-government ♣ inferred present ♥ non-Muslim people in the countryside, people of the towns (i.e. merchant class?) "Unlike his father, Sunni Baru refused to declare himself a Muslim and at once made it clear that he was going to side entirely with the non-Muslim people in the countryside. ... But the people of the towns were too powerful to be treated in this way. They feared that they would lose power and influence, and that their trade would suffer. They found a rebellious leader in Muhammad Turay."[446] "Askia Muhammad made a sharp break with the religious and family traditions of the Sunni line of rulers. He based his power firmly on the towns, and, in line with this, ruled as a strict Muslim."[447]
♠ Impeachment ♣ inferred absent ♥

Customary (informal) checks on executive[edit]

♠ Informal critique of executive ♣ inferred present ♥ Even by the 16th CE West African societies were not as stricly divided into classes compared to Europe and Asia. This is why vertical divisions (clans, descent-lines, communities) mattered more than horizontal divisions and "during the sixteenth century and onwards were much more democratic, much more respectful of the rights of individual persons, than were the governments of Europe."[448] "There came a clash between Muslim law and traditional African law."[449]
♠ Informal sanction of executive ♣ inferred absent ♥
♠ Informal constraint on executive ♣ inferred present ♥ General reference to divisions in societies of West Africa "veritical divisions which separated all the people of one clan from the people of other clans, all the inhabitants of one village from their neighbours, all the descenants of one set of ancestors from the descendants of other ancestors. These vertical, or 'up-and-down', divisions were in some ways even more important to West African social development that the horizontal divisions ... and they were already very ancient by the sixteenth century."[450] "There came a clash between Muslim law and traditional African law."[451] For example, after 1591 CE the ulama of Timbuktu "backed the Moroccans against the Songhay rulers."[452]
♠ Informal impeachment ♣ inferred absent ♥

Limits on executive power imposed by religious agents[edit]

♠ Critique of executive by religious agents ♣ inferred present ♥ General reference to diviners and priests. [453] In Mali Empire there were diviners "whose job it was to predict the future."[454] In Mecca, the sharif of Mecca named Askia Muhammad his caliph for Western Sudan.[455] "Askia Mohammed received from the Abbeside Caliph Mulay Abbas a turban, a green skullcap, and a saber to wear around the neck."[456] The cadi was a Muslim judge appointed by the king who "handled mainly common-law misdemeanors, disputes between citizens, or between citizens and foreigners."[457] "A judge's house and a mosque were inviolable sanctuaries. The cadi could with impunity admonish the king; to the princes, he was the respected intercessor whose words were heeded."[458] Askia Mohammed requested a "Sherif, i.e., the descendant of the Prophet" to be sent to live with in the Sudan. Caliph Mulay Abbas obliged and the Sherif became an important figure, "exempt from all the duties of citizenship (taxes, etc.), but they received gifts of impressive value."[459]
♠ Sanction of executive by religious agents ♣ inferred absent ♥
♠ Constraint on executive by religious agents ♣ inferred present ♥ Caliph Mulay Abbas demanded, and received, "all sorts of administrative exemptions for the Sherif".[460] General reference to diviners and priests. [461] In Mali Empire there were diviners "whose job it was to predict the future."[462] After 1591 CE king's secretary and adviser Alfa Askia Lanbar "betrayed some of the Songhay chiefs to the leader of the Moroccan invaders."[463]
♠ Constraint on executive by religious institutions ♣ inferred present ♥ "While largely loyal to the traditional beliefs of the countryside, Sunni Ali had found it wise to make a good many concessions to Islam, although, ... in the case of Timbuktu, he did not hesitate to oppress Muslim leader who failed in their political loyalty to him.[464] Caliph Mulay Abbas demanded, and received, "all sorts of administrative exemptions for the Sherif".[465] General reference to diviners and priests. [466] In Mali Empire there were diviners "whose job it was to predict the future."[467] After the Moroccan invasion of 1591 CE "the ulama of the Songhay towns then tended to accept the Moroccans as their natural Muslim allies against the non-Muslim people of the Songhay countryside."[468] ulama of Timbuktu "backed the Moroccans against the Songhay rulers."[469]
♠ Impeachment by religious agents ♣ inferred absent ♥
♠ Subordination of the chief executive to a religious agent ♣ absent ♥
♠ Motivation by belief in supernatural agent ♣ present ♥ The Askia's enthronement "was followed by the swearing of oaths. Generals, soldiers, all the people, even the clergy, had to swear on the Koran an oath of faithfulness and obedience to the new Askia. ... The king had to be present at the Friday prayer, which was said in his name."[470]

"Like the Europeans of the Middle Ages (AD 600-1350), Africans lived in an 'age of faith'. They believed, in short, that political authority came not from men or women but from God and the spirits. Those who exercised power on earth could do so, in other words, only if they were accepted as speaking and acting with the good will of their departed ancestors, who, in turn, were their protectors and helpers in the world of the spirits. Rulers could only rule if they were spiritually appointed to do so; and their subjects obeyed them not simply from respect for the king's power and law, but also for reasons of religion."[471]

Mechanisms of Power Transfer (from one head of state to the next)[edit]

♠ Customary mechanism ♣ present ♥
♠ Legal mechanism ♣ absent ♥
♠ Number of power transitions ♣ 10 ♥ 0. Sunni Baru. 1. Askia (1493-1528 CE) 2. Musa (1528-1531 CE) 3. Muhammad Bunkan (1531-1537 CE) 4. Ismail (1537-1539 CE) 5. Ishaq I (1539-1549 CE) 6. Dawud (1549-1582 CE) 7. Muhammad II (1582-1586 CE) 8. Muhammad Bani (1586-1588 CE) 9. Ishaq II (1588-1591 CE) 10. Muhammad Gao (1591-?).[472]
♠ Number of contested transitions ♣ 5 ♥ Sunni Ali was replaced by his son Sunni Baru who was deposed by a rebel called Muhammad Turay (Askia the Great).[473] Askia Muhammad deposed by son Musa 1528 CE.[474] Askia Ishaq deposed in a coup.[475] Askia El Hadj (Muhammad II?) 1582 CE successfully put down a palace revolt.[476] Unsuccessful revolt against Askia Ishaq II.[477]
♠ Number of successful overturns ♣ 3 ♥ Sunni Ali was replaced by his son Sunni Baru who was deposed by a rabel called Muhammad Turay (Askia the Great).[478] Askia Muhammad deposed by son Musa 1528 CE.[479] Askia Ishaq deposed in a coup.[480]

Central Bureaucracy[edit]

♠ State-level administrators ♣ present ♥

Like the Mali Empire that preceded it the Songhay state was run on the resources of slaves tribes and on a system of customs duties and taxation. The ruling family had 24 slave tribes: they were functionally specialised and tasked to produce crops to feed king's army, fodder for his horses, or grow or farm produce, like fish; still other tribes were blacksmiths ("of foreign extraction"); five tribes were obliged to provide "one hundred spears and one hundred arrows per family each year" for the army; from one tribe the king recruited "domestic servants and confidential special emissaries" (but otherwise was exempt from a tax).[481] The slave tribes "had all the external appearances of a clan or tribe, as to number, identity of name, and collateral relationships. Yet they totally lacked the social structure and organization of the clan."[482] Plantations worked by slaves were managed by a fanfa (slave-master or ship-master) who might be responsible for 100, 60, 50, 40, or 20 slaves.[483] A plantation in Dendi had 200 slaves and four fanfas under a leader called Missakulallah, who was also a slave.[484] The state gained tribute from conquered peoples, such as Maghcharen-Koi and Andassen-Koi Berbers who "each were obliged to provide 12,000 soldiers in case of war"[485] and the Katsina and Hausa states.[486] Revenue was gained from taxation on merchants in gold coins or goods[487] and from customs duties on imports and exports, which had existed from the Ghanaian period.[488] In general terms, the West African states were ruled by a non-landowning who nobility lived on "the institution of a tax, first conceived of as a tithe, a ritual deduction on the wealth of all subjects".[489]

The bureaucracy was closely linked to the court and under the Songhai Askias became very well developed. Each high official, directly appointed by the king[490], was given a "distinctive uniform and insignia" which were worn at royal audiences, and they sat together in a highly-formalised assembly.[491] The king wore a headdress at royal audiences[492] and his rank obligated him to speak softly so "the Mansa of Mali, like the Askia, and present-day marabouts, all had their heralds who audibly transmitted to the assembly the words of the chief."[493] Court ceremony very similar throughout Black Africa: for example, covering the head with dust presented a sign of humility.[494]

Written language was the culture of an urban élite, that did not absorb surrounding cultures and languages.[495] The development of the bureaucracy was thus linked to arabic writing and the acceptance of Islamic religion. The oldest example of writing in West Africa is from a 1100 CE tomb inscription at Gao.[496] "Islam provided the kind of laws that were useful to bigger states and larger market-towns. Such laws were about the administration of justice, the inheritance of property, the improvement of market organization".[497] Thus in the Western Sudan "the customs of Islam helped towards the formation of regular civil services staffed by 'king's men': by officials, that is, who owed their appointments not to their birth but to individual skill and service to the king."[498] In addition to Islam, the literate bureaucracy also was long encouraged by merchant habits. "The customs activities of the border areas, based on what we have just said, would presuppose some kind of precise accounting, just as the payment of taxes and other fees would seem to imply the issuing of receipts, especially to the peripatetic merchants at the markets of Timbuktu, Djenne, etc."[499] Given the wealth possessed by merchants perhaps it is noteworthy that: "Appointments to the various offices, in the traditional kingdoms, entailed the payment of a customary fee, not, however, necessarily to the king. ... Later on, with the profanation of the royal office, its secularization, this fee would return to the king. Thus, in Songhai, at the time of the conflict with Morocco, a Fondoko upon appointment was required to turn over two thousand cows."[500]

The extent of the government developed by Songhai Askias can be appreciated in the fact there were more civil chiefs than military ones[501] and that Askia Muhammad was "absent on a pilgrimage to Mecca for more than two years (1494-97) without causing himself any trouble at home."[502] Officials included a finance officer (katisi-farma)and sub-officials responsible for wages, property and expenditure; and a minister of justice (asari-mundia).[503] Other offices at various levels of government included: Guimi-koi or Gumei-koi "port director"[504]; Hi-koi "responsible for ships and smaller craft"[505]; Yobu-koi "in charge of the market"[506]; assara-mundio "kind of police commissioner" found in Djenne and Timbuktu[507]. Sources reported that Askia Ishaq I employed a tax collection agent[508] and Askia Mohammed had a secretary who could draw up written documents.[509] Letter writing was common means of communication[510], hence: "Un important service de courrier relie la cour aux différentes villes et chef-lieux assurant la transmission des ordres de l'Askia."[511] The state had prisons at Kanato, Kabara (near Timbuktu) and elsewhere[512] where there was widespread use of "notarized documents" such as for an inventory of goods belonging to a prison inmate.[513]

♠ Prestige of state-level bureaucracy ♣ equal ♥

♠ State salary ♣ inferred present ♥ Mali and Songhhai kings recruited "domestic servants and confidential special emissaries" from a slave tribe.[521] However, that does not mean they worked for nothing as they would have required maintenance. In fact, these were likely highly respected positions. The king of Mali "selected his domestic servants from among them. When one of the men wished to marry, the king would furnish a dowry of forty thousand cowries 'to the parents-in-law of the groom, so as to prevent the wife or children from claiming their freedom, and in order to make sure that they and their progeny would always remain the property of the Malli-koi'".[522] High officials who were not slaves could have been paid in gold coins.

Local-level officials (provincial, regional, civic administration)[edit]

♠ Number of regional administrative units ♣ 5 ♥

Provinces of Songhay: Kurmina, Dendi, Baro, Dirma, Bangu.

Suni Ali (1464-1492 CE) "divided Songhay into provinces and had appointed governors and commanders with staffs of their own."[537] Askia Muhammad (1493-1526 CE) developed this further.[538] Five provinces, governors called fari or farma or koy.[539] The Most senior governor called the kurminafari.[540]

Songhai "was divided into provinces, cantons, villages, large cities of commercial character such as Djenne and Timbuktu, border areas which were strongholds such as Teghezza, Ualata, Nema, etc."[541]

Askia Muhammad founded a colony in Air (oases that was formerly inhabited by Taureg) and "Many Songhay were settled around the ancient marget of Agades".[542]

♠ Local-level administrators ♣ present ♥

"Each provincial governor in Black Africa was an image of the king, with his own small court."[543] The rulers of West African states and empires: "governed through a system of governors and sub-governors; and each of these had his own bureaucracy ... These office-holders or officials operated an early form of civil service. ... This started a long time ago, certainly in the days of Ancient Ghana."[544] In Songhai "provincial governors were mere civil servants, dismissable at any moment ... but they might remain in office for more than ten years, in fact for life, as long as they did not visibly nuture ambitions above their stations, and their administration was properly conducted."[545] Askia "claimed to have consulted the Almighty in order to find out whom he should name to lead the people of Dendi, and appointed Ali Dudo, who had presumably received the divine approval."[546] Askia Muhammad founded a colony in Air (oases that was formerly inhabited by Taureg) and "Many Songhay were settled around the ancient marget of Agades".[547]

Suni Ali (1464-1492 CE) "divided Songhay into provinces and had appointed governors and commanders with staffs of their own."[548] Askia Muhammad (1493-1526 CE) developed this further.[549] Five provinces, governors called fari or farma or koy.[550] The Most senior governor called the kurminafari.[551] Timbuktu was governed by the Timbuktu-Koi.[552] A koira-banda mundio was a "suburban administrator of a city."[553]

Legal System[edit]

♠ Multiple legal systems ♣ present ♥ Islamic and traditional African law.

"Islam provided the kind of laws that were useful to bigger states and larger market-towns. Such laws were about the administration of justice, the inheritance of property, the improvement of market organization".[571]

"There came a clash between Muslim law and traditional African law."[572]

anfara-kuma "traditional judge in pre-Islamic times. It was a hereditary office always held by members of the Kuma clan. They were anfaras, or judges, whence the term anfara-kuma which came to mean the cadi in Songhai.[573]

The cadi was a Muslim judge appointed by the king who "handled mainly common-law misdemeanors, disputes between citizens, or between citizens and foreigners.[574]

Accessibility of Courts

♠ Courts in polity capital ♣ present ♥ "Islam provided the kind of laws that were useful to bigger states and larger market-towns. Such laws were about the administration of justice, the inheritance of property, the improvement of market organization".[602] The Tarik el Fettach said Askia Mohammed "appointed all the cadis in his kingdom: 'Thus it was that he appointed a cadi at Timbuktu, a cadi in the city of Djenne, and a cadi in each city of his territory which had one, from the Kanta to the Sibiridugu."[603] The Tarikh es Sudan, however, claimed the cadis of Timbuktu was the most important judge and that he could remove those subordinate him[604] - perhaps referring to the period before the centralizing reforms of Askia Mohammed?
♠ Courts in regional capitals ♣ present ♥ "Islam provided the kind of laws that were useful to bigger states and larger market-towns. Such laws were about the administration of justice, the inheritance of property, the improvement of market organization".[605] The Tarik el Fettach said Askia Mohammed "appointed all the cadis in his kingdom: 'Thus it was that he appointed a cadi at Timbuktu, a cadi in the city of Djenne, and a cadi in each city of his territory which had one, from the Kanta to the Sibiridugu."[606] The Tarikh es Sudan, however, claimed the cadis of Timbuktu was the most important judge and that he could remove those subordinate him[607] - perhaps referring to the period before the centralizing reforms of Askia Mohammed?

They were anfaras, or judges, whence the term anfara-kuma which came to mean the cadi in Songhai.[608] The Tarik el Fettach said Askia Mohammed "appointed all the cadis in his kingdom: 'Thus it was that he appointed a cadi at Timbuktu, a cadi in the city of Djenne, and a cadi in each city of his territory which had one, from the Kanta to the Sibiridugu."[609] The Tarikh es Sudan, however, claimed the cadis of Timbuktu was the most important judge and that he could remove those subordinate him[610] - perhaps referring to the period before the centralizing reforms of Askia Mohammed?

Informal Justice[edit]

♠ Non-state judiciary ♣ inferred absent ♥

anfara-kuma: "traditional judge in pre-Islamic times. It was a hereditary office always held by members of the Kuma clan. They were anfaras, or judges, whence the term anfara-kuma which came to mean the cadi in Songhai.[611]

The cadi was a Muslim judge appointed by the king who "handled mainly common-law misdemeanors, disputes between citizens, or between citizens and foreigners.[612] The Tarik el Fettach said Askia Mohammed "appointed all the cadis in his kingdom: 'Thus it was that he appointed a cadi at Timbuktu, a cadi in the city of Djenne, and a cadi in each city of his territory which had one, from the Kanta to the Sibiridugu."[613] "Islam provided the kind of laws that were useful to bigger states and larger market-towns. Such laws were about the administration of justice, the inheritance of property, the improvement of market organization".[614]


♠ Religious judiciary ♣ present ♥

The cadi was a Muslim judge appointed by the king who "handled mainly common-law misdemeanors, disputes between citizens, or between citizens and foreigners.[615] The Tarik el Fettach said Askia Mohammed "appointed all the cadis in his kingdom: 'Thus it was that he appointed a cadi at Timbuktu, a cadi in the city of Djenne, and a cadi in each city of his territory which had one, from the Kanta to the Sibiridugu."[616] "Islam provided the kind of laws that were useful to bigger states and larger market-towns. Such laws were about the administration of justice, the inheritance of property, the improvement of market organization".[617]

Property Rights[edit]

♠ Private property (land) ♣ inferred present ♥ Minister of property called uanei-farma.[618] "The African king, however powerful, was easily persuaded that the soil did not belong to him".[619] "The sale of land, properly speaking, seems to have been unknown in traditional pre-colonial Africa. ... Instead of land having constituted a wealth beyond the reach of certain social categories, it was within everyone's grasp, with no need to forfeit one's freedom, like the serf bound to the soil, in order to make use of it, to 'possess' it. The slave had his own patch of ground; the stranger who just this morning came to the village would also get his. Expropriation of the sort seen in sixteenth-century Europe was unthinkable in the history of precolonnial Africa."[620] "Islam provided the kind of laws that were useful to bigger states and larger market-towns. Such laws were about the administration of justice, the inheritance of property, the improvement of market organization".[621]

Discrimination on the basis of social class (include de facto limitations even when there are no explicit legal restrictions)

♠ Bureaucratic positions are open to people from all social classes ♣ present ♥ Askia Muhammad "opened the way for men of talent in royal service who were not necessarily members of leading descent-lines."[654] Especially from Songhay Askia the Great: "the rise of the 'king's men' ... appointed officials who were not members of the 'big families', and who were loyal only to the king their master."[655] "The generals in Songhai were not necessarily slaves; they could be men of any class; likewise, the civil servants."[656] El Amin was a "simple hostler" whom was promoted by Askia Ismael to "chief of pedestrians, or routemaster" then under Askia Daud became "Djenne-Monzo or chief of the city of Djenne. Rising from the position of hostler to that of governor of one of the largest trading cities in the empire, can truly be called coming up in the world."[657]
♠ Promotion in administrative positions is open to people from all social classes ♣ present ♥ Askia Muhammad "opened the way for men of talent in royal service who were not necessarily members of leading descent-lines."[658] Especially from Songhay Askia the Great: "the rise of the 'king's men' ... appointed officials who were not members of the 'big families', and who were loyal only to the king their master."[659] "The generals in Songhai were not necessarily slaves; they could be men of any class; likewise, the civil servants."[660] El Amin was a "simple hostler" whom was promoted by Askia Ismael to "chief of pedestrians, or routemaster" then under Askia Daud became "Djenne-Monzo or chief of the city of Djenne. Social Mobility[edit]

Status[edit]

Elite status

♠ elite status is hereditary ♣ present ♥ Askia Muhammad "opened the way for men of talent in royal service who were not necessarily members of leading descent-lines."[662] Especially from Songhay Askia the Great: "the rise of the 'king's men' ... appointed officials who were not members of the 'big families', and who were loyal only to the king their master."[663] However many appointments still went to sons, e.g. son of king or son of high official.[664]

Rising from the position of hostler to that of governor of one of the largest trading cities in the empire, can truly be called coming up in the world."[667]

General reference for West African society: Unfree men "usually 'tied' to a certain place and occupation, and were forbidden to move away or change their work."[673]

According to Idrisi gold dust was "the principal product of the land of the Blacks; great and small, they make it their livelihood."[674]

"the division of labor reflected by the caste system did not allow the manufacture of everything that was needed: all one could do was work in the craft allowed for his caste. For everything else, they had to turn to the open market ... each caste monopolized an economic activity, santified by tradition."[675]

Slavery[edit]

♠ Proportion of population enslaved ♣ ♥

"When Askia Mohammed defeated Sonni Baro Dau, the son of Sonni Ali, he took over some twenty-four slave tribes that belonged to him."[676] As retold by Diop, according to the Tarikh el Fettach, "Before belonging to the Songhai, they too had first been the property of the king of Mali; starting only with Sonni Madogo did they change imperial masters."[677]

"The first three tribes were of Bambara origin and had most likely not yet been converte to Islam, if Kati is to be believed; in other words, the slave might be of a religion other than that of his master. The king of Mali selected his domestic servants from among them. When one of the men wished to marry, the king would furnish a dowry of forty thousand cowries 'to the parents-in-law of the groom, so as to prevent the wife or children from claiming their freedom, and in order to make sure that they and their progeny would always remain the property of the Malli-koi'".[678] Slave tribes 'obliged to pay an annual tribute of forty cubits [of cultivated land] per couple, man and wife'[679]

Askia Daud had plantations worked by slaves and managed by a fanfa (slave-master or ship-master) who might be responsible for 100, 60, 50, 40, or 20 slaves.[680] A plantation in Dendi had 200 slaves and four fanfas under a leader called Missakulallah, who was also a slave.[681] However, the slave was not bound to the soil[682] "as long as they remained within the area; they were, more and more, sort of 'subjects' of the king. Their misfortune lay in the awful and hateful fact that they might at any moment be sold."[683] That is the only sense in which they relate to the European slave of the time.[684]

"A freed slave was entitled to a dead of manumission drawn up in proper legal form."[685]

Generally in West African states: slaves sourced through warfare e.g. Muslim state raids non-muslim state for slaves, and vice versa.[686]

Leo Africanus notes the existence of a slave market in Gao, many whom were brought by Norther Africans (i.e. exported).[687]

General reference to increasing stratification of societies in West Africa: "unfree servants or slaves" being the third tier of stratified society, below "free farmers" and "senior chiefs".[688]

In Songhay unfree women "forbidden to marry free men."[689] Askia permitted unfree women to marry free men but stipulated that her children would remain enslaved.[690]

Generally in West African states (except Berber Mauritania): "Seldom if ever was it chattel slavery, ... Often it is scarcely deserved the name of slavery at all, being only a form of service which carried special duties and obligations. Slaves could easily work themselves into freedom."[691]

"The African king, however powerful, was easily persuaded that the soil did not belong to him".[692] "The sale of land, properly speaking, seems to have been unknown in traditional pre-colonial Africa. ... Instead of land having constituted a wealth beyond the reach of certain social categories, it was within everyone's grasp, with no need to forfeit one's freedom, like the serf bound to the soil, in order to make use of it, to 'possess' it. The slave had his own patch of ground; the stranger who just this morning came to the village would also get his. Expropriation of the sort seen in sixteenth-century Europe was unthinkable in the history of precolonnial Africa."[693]

"the Koran's point of view on this question is shaded. One may have a slave under the following conditions: to begin with, if he is a prisoner of a holy war - but then he must be educated, cared for, and converted; but, on the other hand, it is forbidden to take as a slave a Muslim as well educated as oneself; so a slave must be freed as soon as he reaches the intellectual level of his master."[694]

♠ Proportion of population enserfed ♣ 0 ♥ Even by the 16th CE West African societies were not as stricly divided into classes compared to Europe and Asia. This is why vertical divisions (clans, descent-lines, communities) mattered more than horizontal divisions and "during the sixteenth century and onwards were much more democratic, much more respectful of the rights of individual persons, than were the governments of Europe."[695] There was no land holding class in 16th century West Africa.[696]

However, "many big political systems were founded" on a system of vassals (free men) who owed service to their lords who gave them protection.[697]

General reference for West African society: Unfree men "usually 'tied' to a certain place and occupation, and were forbidden to move away or change their work."[698]

General reference: "The African peasant's situation was therefore diametrically opposed to that of the serf bound to the soil and belonging, along with the land he cultivated, to a lord and master. The conditions under which the very first 'masters of the soil,' such as the Serer Lamans, ceded plots of land were in no way comparable to those in force during fuedal times: they could never result in the loss of liberty for a non-slave. Under the worst of them, they caled for an annual rental guaranteed by verbal agreement, cancellable at the end of each season. Even the poor worker, the navetane, who possessed only the strength of his own arms, could not be reduced to slavery. In the morning he worked for the Laman, and in the afternoon for himself, on the same plot granted to him."[699]
